Schallert: Suppose we were to pursue this question of order, the next step, and were to say that peace, harmony - like the harmony of my fingers working together or the harmony of you and me in our dialogue - if we were to say that peace or harmony is the tranquillity that is associated with order and wanted to say ‘what more do we mean by order than just orderliness?’

Krishnamurti: Ah, orderliness every housewife has!

Schallert: Yes, and can be in complete turmoil while having orderliness.

Krishnamurti: We are talking not only of outward order but of deep, inward order.

Schallert: Yes, now what does this deep, inward order - can I use the word ‘ordination’ rather than order?

Krishnamurti: Ordination. I don’t know quite what...

Schallert: The ordination of one to another, then subtract the divisions.

Krishnamurti: If we understand by the word ‘order’ the sense of no conflict, no sense of me being greater than you, no comparison, no sense of ambition, greed - the real quality of mind that is not concerned with all this bilge, all this nonsense. Then that is order.

Schallert: Then that order and peace and tranquillity is energy in its fullness rather than the lack of energy. It’s not activity. It is the fullness of energy, so it’s dynamic.

Krishnamurti: Yes, and that is necessary, isn’t it? Which means when there is that complete order, the mind is no longer in conflict, and therefore has an abundance of energy.

Schallert: And what has been done by you or by me as we related to each other in order to achieve this order?

Krishnamurti: You can’t achieve it. Out of being aware of disorder, choicelessly, order comes naturally.

Schallert: But it is true that many people do not achieve order - and we were also asking the question: can we change disorder into order or can we change death into life, can we change hate into love, can we change blindness into seeing? These are the questions we’ve been dealing with and we haven’t answered the question: can this change take place?

Krishnamurti: I, or you, listen to what is being said. You give your whole attention, not as a Catholic or this or that. Now in that state of attention, there is a transformation. You’re no longer a Hindu, Buddhist or whatever it is. You’re finished with all that, you are now a total human being. Then you go around talking about it - you follow? You’re acting, you’re an outsider operating on the world. You’re not of the world but an outsider.

Schallert: Would you say that, in our conversation, that the closer we get to the truth, the less conscious you become of the fact that I am a Catholic priest? Does it matter?

Krishnamurti: Not in the least. But it’s up to you.

Schallert: And it does not matter to me whether you are a priest or not. I haven’t thought of that because of giving choiceless attention to you.

Krishnamurti: It makes this difference. For instance, I’ve met Hindus in India who have come to me and said, ‘Why don’t you put on sannyasi robes?’ You know, monk’s robes. I said, ‘Why should I?’ ‘To show that you are not of the world.’ I said, ‘Look, I don’t want to show anything to anybody. This is revealed to me, that’s good enough. If you want to come and listen, listen, but don’t go by my garb, my gesture, my face, that’s not important.’ But to them it is important because they use that as a platform from which to attack or distract or take. But if you’re not standing on any platform; if you don’t belong to anything, why should I wear a collar, or no collar, or no shirt?

Schallert: As we pursue the question of what it means to live and to die, to be and to not be, to love and to hate - as we pursue those things - we must at the same time pursue the question of what it means to belong. Now, if you asked me, ‘Do you belong to the Catholic church?’ I would say, ‘Of course not’, because I am not a thing that can be possessed by anyone.

Krishnamurti: Quite.

Schallert: Nor is the Catholic church something that I possess, so I would not like to use the word belong. If we had a loving relationship with each other, could I say, ‘You are my friend’?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Schallert: No, I couldn’t because that would connote belonging!

Krishnamurti: I see what you mean...

Schallert: I could not say you were my friend. We use the word all the time, but the word ‘my’ distorts what we see...

Krishnamurti: I’m questioning: why do we belong to anything at all?

Schallert: I don’t think we can. If we are free, then we’re not slaves and we don’t belong to anything.

Krishnamurti: That is the main thing.

Schallert: Possessive relationship is irrelevant.

Krishnamurti: Not to belong to any organized, spiritual, religious group or belong to a party, this or that, because that encourages division.

Schallert: If I am or if I am free - they mean the same thing - then I’m not capable of being possessed by anyone, I don’t belong. The word doesn’t mean anything.

Krishnamurti: Not to belong means to stand alone.

Schallert: Belonging is in contradiction to what we’ve been saying the whole time. ‘Not to belong’ is the price one must pay for being and loving and seeing anything.

Krishnamurti: Yes, sir, but also it implies not to belong to any structure which human beings have put together.

Schallert: Yes.

Krishnamurti: Which means that you have to stand alone, outside, not belong to all these messes. Sir, when you have order, you don’t belong to disorder.

Schallert: Now I think we’re getting close to what we wanted to say, that to die is to live and not to belong.

Krishnamurti: Is that a concept or a reality?

Schallert: No, that’s an experience, it’s a reality, yes.

Krishnamurti: If it is real, it’s something burning! It burns everything false!

Schallert: I see that. And of course we experience this all the time. What I’m saying is that if one can get over the fear of dying then one can understand and live with the fullness of this energy that we’re talking about. I think by the same token, if one can get over the question of belonging or having in any way, one can get to the question that being is. And I wonder if this is being alone.

Krishnamurti: The danger of being, one has to go into that. What is it to be? You can put it into various categories. The category is not being.

Schallert: But when we were discussing the question of what it is to be and we pursued this through the question of dying and belonging and you said, ‘to be is to be alone and...’

Krishnamurti: How can I, sir, if I die to my conditioning as a Hindu, how can I be a Hindu? It has no meaning!

Schallert: Fine, but having died to...

Krishnamurti: See what happens, sir. I discard, I throw away the garb of Hinduism or Catholicism, whatever it is, and what takes place? I am an outsider. I’m an outsider in the sense that I may say I love you, but I’m still an outsider because there is a state of disorder to which human beings belong and the man who is outside just doesn’t belong.

Schallert: Unquestionably. Or may not have a sense of belonging, or cannot use the word ‘to belong’. I cannot use the word ‘belong’.

Krishnamurti: There is no relationship.

Schallert: There is no relationship.

Krishnamurti: Now, when there is no relationship between disorder and order, what is the state of the mind which is not that disorder?

Schallert: And you were suggesting that the state of the mind is one of being alone.

Krishnamurti: Alone in the sense that it is not contaminated, it is really innocent. It is really innocent in the sense that it cannot be hurt. After all, the root meaning of that word is not to be hurt. So though it may live in the world, it is not of the world.

Schallert: In the sense of conflict and turmoil.

Krishnamurti: All the messy stuff. That is absolutely necessary to find out more - not more in the sense of something more - but that state is absolutely essential to discover whether the immeasurable is or not.

Schallert: Yes, I think this is true, so that we do, in a sense, find seeing and loving and being in being alone. One disassociates oneself from disorder.

Krishnamurti: In observing disorder, in being aware choicelessly of disorder, order comes. You don’t belong - there is order.

Schallert: Order. And as we pursued the question of the meaning of order or harmony or tranquillity or peace, we found ourselves with the same answer, but this is to be in the first place, this is to love in the first place, this is to see in the first place.

Krishnamurti: Order, sir, is one of the most extraordinary things because it’s always new. It isn’t order according to a pattern, it is a living thing. Virtue is a living thing. It isn’t: ‘I am virtuous’. You can never say I’m virtuous because if you say that you’re not virtuous. But virtue is a living thing like a flowing river, it is alive, and therefore in that state something beyond measure takes place.

Schallert: And it is at that moment that one discovers the immeasurable.

Krishnamurti: Yes, not discover: it is there. You see, discovery and experience are rather unfortunate words because most human beings want to experience something great, their lives are shoddy, petty, full of anxiety and they say, for God’s sake, give me greater experience, something more! Therefore you have these groups that meditate and so forth, they’re searching for that, whereas they have to bring order in their lives first, and then what takes place is something quite beyond measure.

Schallert: So if we are pursuing the question of measurelessness...

Krishnamurti: You can’t pursue it.

Schallert: And you can’t discover it. Fine, this is good. You cannot pursue it, you cannot discover it, and it’s not good to use the word ‘experience’ about it - all of this we put aside. When one comes upon it...

Krishnamurti: You leave the door open, sir.

Schallert: You leave the door open.

Krishnamurti: And let the sun come in. And if the sun comes in, it’s all right, if it doesn’t, it’s all right. Because the moment you pursue it you close the door.

Schallert: Good, the very pursuit is...

Krishnamurti: Then the very search for truth is the closing off, the blocking of truth.

Brockwood Park, 11 June 1983

San Diego, California, 17 February 1972

What Future Does Man Have?

David Bohm, FRS

Krishnamurti: I thought we should talk about the future of man. As things are, from what one observes, the world has become tremendously dangerous. There are terrorists, wars, and national and racial divisions, some dictators who want to destroy the world and so on. Also, religiously there is tremendous separation.

David Bohm: And there is the economic crisis and the ecological crisis.

Krishnamurti: Problems seem to be multiplying more and more. So what is the future of man? What is the future not only of the present generation but of the coming generations?

Bohm: Well, the future looks very grim.

Krishnamurti: Yes, if you and I were quite young, and knowing all this, what would we do? What would be our reaction? What would be our life, our way of earning a livelihood and so on?

Bohm: I have often thought about that. I have asked myself, ‘would I go into science again?’ And I am not at all certain now because science does not seem to be relevant to this crisis.

Krishnamurti: No, on the contrary it is helping to...

Bohm: ...make it worse. Science might help but in fact it isn’t.

Krishnamurti: So, what would you do? I think I would stick to what I am doing.

Bohm: Well, that would be easy for you.

Krishnamurti: For me, quite easy. You see, I don’t think in terms of evolution.

Bohm: I was expecting we would discuss that.

Krishnamurti: I don’t think there is psychological evolution at all.

Bohm: We have often discussed this, so I understand to some extent what you mean. But I think that people new to this are not going to understand.

Krishnamurti: Yes, we will discuss this whole question, if you will. But why are we concerned about the future? The whole future is now.

Bohm: Well, in some sense the whole future is now but we have to make that clear. This goes very much against the whole traditional way of thinking.

Krishnamurti: Yes, I know. Mankind thinks in terms of evolution, continuance, and so on.

Bohm: Maybe we could approach it in another way. That is, evolution seems in the present era to be the most natural way to think. So I would like to ask you: what objections do you have to thinking in terms of evolution? The word has, of course, many meanings.

Krishnamurti: Of course, we are talking psychologically.

Bohm: Yes, now the first point is: let’s dispose of it physically.

Krishnamurti: An acorn will grow into an oak.

Bohm: Yes, also the species has evolved; for example, from plants to animals and to man.

Krishnamurti: Yes, we have taken a million years to be what we are.

Bohm: You do not doubt that that has happened?

Krishnamurti: No, that has happened.

Bohm: It may continue to happen. That is a valid process.

Krishnamurti: That is evolution. Of course, that is a valid, natural process.

Bohm: It takes place in time and therefore in that region the past, present and future are important.

Krishnamurti: Yes, obviously. I don’t know a certain language, and I need time to learn it.

Bohm: It also takes time to improve the brain. If the brain was small when it started out, it got steadily larger, and that took a million years.

Krishnamurti: Yes, and it becomes much more complex and so on. All that needs time, all that is movement in space and time.

Bohm: So you will admit physical time and neurophysiological time.

Krishnamurti: Absolutely, of course, any sane man would.

Bohm: Now, most people also admit psychological time, what they call mental time.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is what we are talking about. Whether there is such a thing as psychological tomorrow, psychological evolution.

Bohm: Now, at first sight I am afraid this will sound strange. It seems I can remember yesterday, and there is tomorrow, I can anticipate. It has happened many times, you know that days have succeeded each other. So I do have the experience of time, from yesterday to today to tomorrow - right?

Krishnamurti: Of course, that is simple enough.

Bohm: Now what is it that you are denying?

Krishnamurti: I deny that I will be something, become better.

Bohm: But there are two ways of looking at that. One way is: will I intentionally become better because I am trying? Or some people feel that evolution is a kind of natural, inevitable process, which is sweeping us along like a current, and we are perhaps becoming better or worse, or something else is happening to us.

Krishnamurti: Psychologically.

Bohm: Yes, which takes time but which may not be the result of my trying to become better. It may or may not be, some people may think one way, some another. But are you denying that there is a kind of natural psychological evolution like the natural biological evolution?

Krishnamurti: I am denying that, yes.

Bohm: Well, why do you deny it?

Krishnamurti: First of all, what is the psyche, the ‘me’, the ego, and so on, what is it?

Bohm: Well, the word ‘psyche’ has many meanings. It may mean the mind, for example. Do you mean that the ego is the same thing?

Krishnamurti: The ego, I am talking of the ego, the ‘me’.

Bohm: Yes, now some people think of evolution as a process in which the ‘me’ will be transcended, that it will rise to a higher level. So there are two questions. One is: will the ‘me’ ever improve? And the other question is: supposing we want to get beyond the ‘me’, can that be done in time?

Krishnamurti: That cannot be done in time.

Bohm: Yes, now we have to make it clear why not.

Krishnamurti: I will, we will go into it. What is the ‘me’? If the word ‘psyche’ has such different meanings, the ‘me’ is the whole movement which thought has brought about.

Bohm: Why do you say that?

Krishnamurti: The ‘me’ is the consciousness, my consciousness, the ‘me’ is my name, form and all the various experiences that I have had, remembrances and so on. The whole structure of the ‘me’ is put together by thought.

Bohm: Well, that again would be something which some people might find hard to accept.

Krishnamurti: Of course, we are discussing it.

Bohm: Let us try to bring it out. Because the first experience, the first feeling I have about the ‘me’ is that it is there independently and that the ‘me’ is thinking.

Krishnamurti: Is the ‘me’ independent of my thinking?

Bohm: Well, my own first feeling is that the ‘me’ is there independent of my thinking, and it is the ‘me’ that is thinking.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: Just as I am here and can move my arm or head, I can think. Now is that an illusion?

Krishnamurti: No, because when I move my arm, there is an intention to grasp or pick up something, which means that first it is the movement of thought. That makes the arm move and so on. My contention - and I am open to this being challenged - is that thought is the basis of all this.

Bohm: Yes, your contention is that the whole sense of the ‘me’ and what it is doing is coming out of thought. What you mean by thought, though, is not merely intellectual.

Krishnamurti: No, of course not. Thought is the whole movement of experience, knowledge, and memory. It is this movement.

Bohm: It sounds to me as if you mean the consciousness as a whole.

Krishnamurti: As a whole, that’s right.

Bohm: And you are saying that this movement is the ‘me’ - right?

Krishnamurti: The whole content of that consciousness is the ‘me’. The ‘me’ is not different from my consciousness.

Bohm: Yes, well, I think one could say that I am my consciousness, for if I am not conscious I am not here. Now, is consciousness nothing but what you have just described, which includes thought, feeling, intention?

Krishnamurti: ...intention, aspirations...

Bohm: ...memories...

Krishnamurti: ...memories, beliefs, dogmas, the rituals that are performed, the whole of that, like a computer that has been programmed.

Bohm: Yes, everybody would agree that certainly is in consciousness, but many people would feel that there is more to it, that consciousness may go beyond that.

Krishnamurti: Let’s go into it. The content of our consciousness makes up the consciousness.

Bohm: I think that requires some clarification. The ordinary use of the word ‘content’ is quite different. If you say that the content of a glass is water, the glass is one thing and the water is another. The glass contains the water, so the word ‘content’ would suggest that something contains it.

Krishnamurti: All right, consciousness is made up of all that it has remembered, beliefs, dogmas, rituals, nationalities, fears, pleasures, sorrow.

Bohm: Yes, now if all that were absent would there be no consciousness?

Krishnamurti: Not as we know it.

Bohm: But there would still be a kind of consciousness?

Krishnamurti: A totally different kind.

Bohm: Well, then I think you really mean to say that consciousness, as we know it, is made up...

Krishnamurti: ...is the result of the multiple activities of thought. Thought has put my consciousness together - the reactions, the responses, the memories, the extraordinary complex intricacies, subtleties - all that is, makes up, consciousness.

Bohm: As we know it.

Krishnamurti: As we know it. The question is whether that consciousness has a future.

Bohm: Does it have a past?

Krishnamurti: Of course, remembrance.

Bohm: Remembrance, yes. Why do you say it has no future then?

Krishnamurti: If it has a future it will be exactly the same kind of thing. The same activities, same thoughts, modified, but the pattern will be repeated over and over again.

Bohm: So are you saying that thought can only repeat?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: But there is a feeling that thought can develop new ideas, for example.

Krishnamurti: But thought is limited because knowledge is limited, if you admit that knowledge will always be limited.

Bohm: Now, why do you say knowledge is always limited?

Krishnamurti: Because you as a scientist are experimenting, adding, searching. You are adding, and after you some other person will add more. So knowledge, which is born of experience, is limited.

Bohm: Some people have argued that it isn’t. They would hope to obtain perfect or absolute knowledge of the laws of nature.

Krishnamurti: The laws of nature are not the laws of human being.

Bohm: Well, do you want to restrict the discussion then to knowledge about the human being?

Krishnamurti: Of course, that’s all we can talk about.

Bohm: All right. So are we saying that man cannot obtain unlimited knowledge of the psyche? Is that what you are saying? There is always more that is unknown.

Krishnamurti: Yes, there is always more that is unknown. So once we admit that knowledge is limited then thought is limited.

Bohm: Yes, thought depends on knowledge and knowledge does not cover everything. Therefore thought will not be able to handle everything that happens.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. But that is what the politicians and all the other people are trying to do. They think that thought can solve every problem.

Bohm: You can see in the case of politicians that knowledge is very limited. And when you lack adequate knowledge of what you are dealing with you create confusion.

Krishnamurti: So then as thought is limited, our consciousness, which has been put together by thought, is limited.

Bohm: Now, can you make that clear? That means we can only stay in the same circle.

Krishnamurti: The same circle.

Bohm: You see, if you compare with science, people might argue that although my knowledge is limited I am constantly discovering.

Krishnamurti: What you discover is added on, but is still limited.

Bohm: It is still limited. That’s the point. I think one of the ideas behind the scientific approach is that although knowledge is limited I can discover and keep up with the actuality.

Krishnamurti: But that is also limited.

Bohm: My discoveries are limited and there is always the unknown that I have not discovered.

Krishnamurti: That is what I am saying. The unknown, the limitless, cannot be captured by thought because thought in itself is limited. So do you and I agree to that, not only agree but see it is a fact?

Bohm: Well, perhaps we could bring it out still more. That is, thought is limited, even though there is a very strong predisposition, feeling, tendency, to feel that thought can do anything.

Krishnamurti: But it can’t. See what it has done in the world!

Bohm: Well, I agree that it has done some terrible things but that doesn’t prove that it is always wrong. You see, maybe you could always blame that on people who have used it wrongly.

Krishnamurti: I know, that is a good old trick! But thought in itself is limited, therefore whatever it does is limited.

Bohm: And you are saying it is limited in a very serious way.

Krishnamurti: That’s right, in a very, very serious way.

Bohm: Well, could we bring that out, and say what that way is?

Krishnamurti: That way is what is happening in the world. The totalitarian ideals are the invention of thought.

Bohm: Yes, we could say that the very word ‘totalitarian’ means they wanted to cover the totality but they couldn’t - and the thing collapsed.

Krishnamurti: It is collapsing.

Bohm: But then there are those who say they are not totalitarians.

Krishnamurti: But the thinking of the democrats, the republicans and the idealists and so on is also limited.

Bohm: Yes, limited in a way that is...

Krishnamurti: ...very destructive.

Bohm: ...that is very serious and destructive. Now in what way could we bring that out? You see, I could say, ‘OK, my thought is limited but this may not be all that serious’. Why is it so important?

Krishnamurti: That is fairly simple: because whatever action is born of limited thought must inevitably breed conflict. Dividing humanity geographically into nationalities and dividing humanity religiously and so on has created havoc in the world.

Bohm: Yes, let’s connect that with the limitation of thought. That is, my knowledge is limited. Now how does that lead me to divide the world?

Krishnamurti: Aren’t we seeking security? We thought there was security in the family, security in the tribe, security in nationalism. So we thought that there was security in division.

Bohm: Yes, that seems to be how it has come out: take the tribe, for example; one may feel insecure and then say, ‘with the tribe I am secure’. That is a conclusion. And I think I know enough to be sure that is so but I don’t. Other things happen that I don’t know of which make that very insecure. Other tribes come along.

Krishnamurti: No, the very division itself creates insecurity.

Bohm: It helps to create it but I am trying to say that I don’t know enough to know that - I don’t see that.

Krishnamurti: One doesn’t see it because one has not looked at the world as a whole.

Bohm: Well, the thought that aims at security attempts to know everything important. As soon as it knows everything important it says, ‘this will bring security’ - yet not only are there a lot of things it doesn’t know but one thing it doesn’t know is that this very thought is itself divisive. It’s going to be divisive, because I define an area that is secure, divided from another area.

Krishnamurti: It is divisive because in itself it is limited. Anything that is limited must inevitably create conflict.

Bohm: Well, you mean any thought that is...

Krishnamurti: If I say I am an individual, it is limited. I am concerned with myself, that is very limited.

Bohm: Yes, we have to get this clear. You see, if I say this is a table, which is limited, it creates no conflict - right?

Krishnamurti: No, there is no conflict there.

Bohm: Now when I say this is ‘me’, that creates conflict.

Krishnamurti: The ‘me’ is a divisive entity.

Bohm: Let’s see more clearly why.

Krishnamurti: Because it is separative, it is concerned with itself; and the ‘me’ identifying with the greater, the nation, is still divisive.

Bohm: Yes, well, I define myself in the interest of security so that I know what I am as opposed to what you are and I protect myself. Now this creates a division between me and you.

Krishnamurti: We and they and so on.

Bohm: We and they. Now that comes from my limited thought, because I don’t understand that we are really closely related and connected.

Krishnamurti: We are human beings.

Bohm: Yes, we are all human beings.

Krishnamurti: All human beings have more or less the same problems.

Bohm: But I haven’t understood that. My knowledge is limited, I think that we can make a distinction and protect ourselves and me and not the others. But in the very act of doing that I create instability, insecurity.

Krishnamurti: That’s right, you create insecurity. So if we see that, not merely intellectually or verbally, but actually feel it, that we are the rest of humanity, then the responsibility becomes immense.

Bohm: Well, how can you do anything about that responsibility?

Krishnamurti: Then I either contribute to the whole mess, or keep out of it. That is, to be at peace, to have order in oneself. I will come to that, I am going too fast.

Bohm: You see, I think we have touched upon an important point. We say the whole of humanity, of mankind, is one, and therefore to create division there is...

Krishnamurti: ...dangerous.

Bohm: Yes, whereas to create division between me and the table is not dangerous because in some sense we are not one. Now mankind doesn’t realize that it is all one.

Krishnamurti: Why, why?

Bohm: Well, let’s go into that. This is a crucial point. It is clear it doesn’t, because there are so many divisions, and not only between nations and religions but between one person and another.

Krishnamurti: Why is there this division?

Bohm: Well, the first thing, at least in the modern era, is the belief that every human being is an individual. This may not have been so strong in the past.

Krishnamurti: That is what I question. I question altogether whether we are individuals.

Bohm: That is a big question because...

Krishnamurti: Of course. We said just now that the consciousness which is ‘me’ is similar to that of all other human beings. They all suffer, they all have fears, they are all insecure, they have their own particular gods and rituals, all put together by thought.

Bohm: Yes, well, I think this calls for some clarification. There are two questions here. One is, not everybody feels that he is similar - most people feel they have some unique distinction.

Krishnamurti: What do you mean by ‘unique distinction’? Distinction in doing something?

Bohm: There may be many things. For example, one nation may feel that it is able to do some things better than another, one person has some special things he does, or a particular quality.

Krishnamurti: Of course, you are more intellectual than I am. Somebody else is better in this or that.

Bohm: He may take pride in his own special abilities or advantages.

Krishnamurti: But when you put that away we are basically the same.

Bohm: You are saying that these things you have just described are...

Krishnamurti: ...superficial.

Bohm: Now, what are the things that are basic?

Krishnamurti: Fear, sorrow, pain, anxiety, loneliness, all the human travail.

Bohm: But many people might feel that the highest achievements of man are the basic things.

Krishnamurti: What have we achieved?

Bohm: For one thing people may feel proud of the achievements of man in science, art, culture and technology.

Krishnamurti: We have achievements in all those directions, certainly we have vast technology, communication, travel, medicines, surgery...

Bohm: It is really remarkable in many ways.

Krishnamurti: There is no question about that. But what have we achieved psychologically?

Bohm: You are saying that none of these achievements has affected us psychologically?

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s right.

Bohm: And the psychological question is more important than any of the others because if the psychological question is not cleared up the rest is dangerous.

Krishnamurti: If we are limited psychologically, then whatever we do will be limited, and the technology will then be used by our limited psyche.

Bohm: Yes, the master is this limited psyche and not the rational structure of technology; and in fact technology then becomes a dangerous...

Krishnamurti: ...instrument.

Bohm: So that is one point: that the psyche is at the core of it all, and if the psyche is not in order then the rest is useless.

Krishnamurti: If the house is in order...

Bohm: Then the second question is this: although we are saying that there are certain basic disorders in the psyche, or a lack of order which is common to us all, and that we may all have a potential for something else, are we really all one? Even though we are all similar, that doesn’t mean that we are all the same, that we are all one.

Krishnamurti: We said that in our consciousness we all have basically the same ground on which we stand.

Bohm: Yes, but the fact that human bodies are similar doesn’t prove they are all the same.

Krishnamurti: Of course not, your body is different from mine.

Bohm: Yes, we are in different places, different entities and so on. But I think you are trying to say that consciousness is not an entity which is individual...

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Bohm: ...the body is an entity that has a certain individuality.

Krishnamurti: That all seems so clear.

Bohm: It may be clear, but I think...

Krishnamurti: Your body is different from mine, I have a different name from you.

Bohm: Well, we are different - though of similar material, it is different, we can’t exchange bodies because the proteins in one body may not agree with those in the other. Now many people feel that way about the mind, saying that there is a chemistry between people which may agree or disagree.

Krishnamurti: But if you actually go deeper into the question, consciousness is shared by all human beings.

Bohm: Yes, but the feeling is that the consciousness is individual and that it is communicated as it were...

Krishnamurti: I think that is an illusion because we are sticking to something that is not true.

Bohm: Well, do you want to say that there is one consciousness of mankind?

Krishnamurti: It is all one.

Bohm: It is all one. That is important because whether it is many or one is a crucial question.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: Now, it could be there are many who are then communicating and building up the larger unit. Or do you think that from the very beginning it is all one?

Krishnamurti: From the very beginning it is all one.

Bohm: And the sense of separateness is an illusion - right?

Krishnamurti: That is what I am saying over and over again. That seems so logical, sane: the other is insane.

Bohm: Now, people don’t feel, at least not immediately, that the notion of separate existence is insane, because one extrapolates from the body to the mind, one says it is quite sensible to say my body is separate from yours, and inside my body is my mind. Now are you saying that the mind is not inside the body?

Krishnamurti: That is quite a different question. Now just a minute, let’s finish with the other first. If each one of us thinks that we are separate individuals psychically, what we have done in the world is a colossal mess.

Bohm: Well, if we think we are separate when we are not separate then it clearly will be a colossal mess.

Krishnamurti: That is what is happening. Each one thinks he has to do what he wants to do, fulfil himself. So he is struggling in his separateness to achieve peace, to achieve security, a security and peace which is totally denied by that.

Bohm: Well, the reason it is denied is because there is no separation. You see if there were really separation it would be a rational thing to try to do. But if we are trying to separate what is inseparable the result will be chaos.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Bohm: Now that is clear but I think that it will not be clear to people immediately that the consciousness of mankind is one inseparable whole.

Krishnamurti: Yes, sir, an inseparable whole, absolutely right.

Bohm: Many questions will arise if you once even consider the notion, but I don’t know if we have gone far enough into this yet. One question is: why do we think we are separate?

Krishnamurti: Why do I think I am separate? That is my conditioning.

Bohm: Yes, but how did we ever adopt such a foolish conditioning?

Krishnamurti: From childhood this is mine, this is my toy, not yours.

Bohm: But the first feeling you get is to say it is mine because I feel I am separate. Now, it isn’t clear how the mind which was one came to this illusion that it is all broken up into many pieces.

Krishnamurti: I think it is again the activity of thought. Thought in its very nature is divisive, fragmentary, and therefore I am a fragment.

Bohm: Thought will create a sense of fragments. You can see, for example, that once we decide to set up a nation we will be separate, think we are separate from another nation, and from that all sorts of things, consequences, follow that make the whole thing seem independently real. We all have a separate language and a separate flag and a separate this and that, and we set up a boundary. And after a while we see so much evidence of separation that we forget how it started and say that was always how it was and we are merely proceeding from what was always there.

Krishnamurti: Of course. That’s why, I feel if once we grasp the nature of thought, the structure of thought, how thought operates, what is the source of thought, and therefore see it is always limited, if we really see that then...

Bohm: Now, what is the source of thought, is it memory?

Krishnamurti: Memory, the remembrance of things past, which is knowledge, and knowledge is the outcome of experience and experience is always limited.

Bohm: But thought also includes, of course, the attempt to go forward, to use logic, to take into account discoveries and insights.

Krishnamurti: And, as we were saying some time ago, thought is time.

Bohm: Yes, all right, thought is time. That requires more discussion too, because you see the first experience is to say that time is there first, and thought is taking place in time.

Krishnamurti: Ah, no.

Bohm: For example, if we say that movement is taking place, the body is moving, and this requires time.

Krishnamurti: To go from here to there needs time, to learn a language needs time, to paint a picture takes time.

Bohm: To grow a plant needs time. We also say to think takes time.

Krishnamurti: So we think in terms of time.

Bohm: You see the first point that one would tend to look at is to say that just as everything takes time, to think takes time. And you are saying something else, which is: thought is time.

Krishnamurti: Thought is time.

Bohm: That is, psychically speaking, psychologically speaking. Now, how do we understand that? That thought is time. You see it is not obvious.

Krishnamurti: Would you say that thought is movement and time is movement?

Bohm: You see, time is a mysterious thing, people have argued about it. We could say that time requires movement. I could understand that we cannot have time without movement.

Krishnamurti: Time is movement, time is not separate from movement.

Bohm: Now, I don’t say it is separate from movement, but to say time is movement, you see if we said time and movement are one...

Krishnamurti: Yes, we are saying that.

Bohm: Yes, they cannot be separated. That seems fairly clear. Now there is physical movement which means physical time - right?

Krishnamurti: Physical time, hot and cold, and also dark and light, sunset and sunrise, all that...

Bohm: The seasons, yes. Now then we have the movement of thought. Now that brings in the question of the nature of thought. You see, is thought nothing but a movement in the nervous system, in the brain? Would you say that?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: Some people have said it includes the movement of the nervous system but there might be something beyond.

Krishnamurti: What is time, actually? Time is hope.

Bohm: Psychologically speaking.

Krishnamurti: Psychologically, I am talking entirely psychologically for the moment. Hope is time, becoming is time, achieving is time. Now take the question of becoming: I want to become something psychologically. I want to become non-violent, take that for example. That is altogether a fallacy.

Bohm: Well, we understand it is a fallacy but the reason it is one is that there is no time of that kind. Is that it?

Krishnamurti: Yes. Human beings are violent: and Tolstoy and people in India, have been talking a great deal of non-violence. The fact is we are violent and the non-violence is not real. But we want to become that.

Bohm: Yes, but again it is an extension of the kind of thought that we have with regard to material things. If you see a desert, it is real and you say the garden is not real, but in your mind is the garden that will come about when you put water there. So we say we can plan for the future when the desert will become fertile. Now we have to be careful: we say we are violent but we cannot by similar planning become non-violent. Now why is that?

Krishnamurti: Why? Because the non-violent state cannot exist when there is violence. That’s an ideal.

Bohm: Well, one has to make it clearer because in the same sense the fertile state and the desert don’t exist together either. You see, I think that you are saying that, in the case of the mind when you are violent, non-violence has no meaning.

Krishnamurti: That is the only state, not the other.

Bohm: That is all there is, the movement towards the other is illusory.

Krishnamurti: Yes, all ideals are psychologically illusory. The ideal of building a marvellous bridge is not illusory. You can plan a bridge but to have psychological ideals...

Bohm: Yes, if you are violent and continue to be violent while you are trying to be non-violent...

Krishnamurti: ...it has no meaning and yet that has become such an important thing. So I question both becoming ‘what is’ and becoming away from ‘what is’.

Bohm: ‘What should be’, yes. Well, if you say there can be no sense to becoming in the way of self-improvement, that’s...

Krishnamurti: Oh, self-improvement is something so utterly ugly. We are saying that the source of all this is a movement of thought as time. Once we have made time important psychologically all the ideals, such as non-violence, achieving some superstate and so on, become utterly illusory.

Bohm: Yes, when you talk of the movement of thought as time, it seems to me that that time is illusory.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: We sense it as time but it is not a real kind of time.

Krishnamurti: That is why we asked: what is time? I need time to go from here to there. I need time if I want to learn engineering, I must study it, that takes time. That same movement is carried over into the psyche. We say, I need time to be good, I need time to be enlightened.

Bohm: Yes, that will always create a conflict. One part of you and another. So that movement in which you say, I need time, also creates a division in the psyche. Between the observer and the observed.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s right. We are saying that the observer is the observed.

Bohm: And psychologically, therefore, there is no time.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. The thinker is the thought, there is no thinker separate from thought.

Bohm: All that you are saying, you know, seems very reasonable, but I think that it goes so strongly against the tradition we are used to that it will be extraordinarily hard, generally speaking, for people to really understand...

Krishnamurti: Of course, most people want a comfortable way of living: ‘let me carry on as I am, for God’s sake, leave me alone.’

Bohm: Yes, that is the result of so much conflict that people are wary.

Krishnamurti: But conflict escaped from or unresolved still exists, whether you like it or not. So this is the whole point: is it possible to live a life without conflict?

Bohm: Yes, that is all implicit in what has been said. The source of conflict is thought or knowledge, or the past.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. So then one asks: is it possible to transcend thought? Or is it possible to end knowledge? I am putting it psychologically, not...

Bohm: Yes, knowledge of material objects and things like that, scientific knowledge, will continue.

Krishnamurti: Absolutely, that must continue.

Bohm: But what you call self-knowledge is what you are asking to end, isn’t it?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: On the other hand people have said, even you have said, self-knowledge is very important.

Krishnamurti: Self-knowledge is important but if I take time to understand myself, that is, if I say I will understand myself eventually by examination, analysis, by watching my whole relationship with others and so on, all that involves time. And I am saying there is another way of looking at the whole thing without time: which is, when the observer is the observed. In that observation there is no time.

Bohm: Could we go into that further? I mean, you say, for example, that there is no time but you still feel that you can remember an hour ago that you were somewhere else. Now in what sense can we say that there is no time?

Krishnamurti: Time is division, as thought is division. That is why thought is time.

Bohm: Time is a series of divisions of past, present, future.

Krishnamurti: Thought is divisive in the same way. So time is thought. Or thought is time.

Bohm: Yes, well, it doesn’t exactly follow from what you said.

Krishnamurti: Let’s go into it.

Bohm: You see, at first sight one would think that thought makes divisions of all kinds, with the ruler and with all kinds of things, and also divides up intervals of time, past, present and future. Now it doesn’t follow just from that that thought is time.

Krishnamurti: Look, we said time is movement. Thought is also a series of movements. So both are movements.

Bohm: Yes, all right. Thought is a movement, we suppose, of the nervous system and...

Krishnamurti: You see, it is a movement of becoming. I am speaking psychologically.

Bohm: But whenever you think, something is also moving in the blood, in the nerves and so on. Now when we talk of a psychological movement, do you mean just a change of content? What is the movement, what is moving?

Krishnamurti: Look, I am this, and I am attempting to become something else psychologically.

Bohm: So that movement is in the content of your thought?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: So if you say, ‘I am this and I am attempting to become that’, then I am in movement. At least I feel I am in movement.

Krishnamurti: Say, for instance, I am greedy. Greed is a movement.

Bohm: What kind of a movement is it?

Krishnamurti: To get what I want, to get more. It is a movement.

Bohm: All right.

Krishnamurti: And I find that movement painful, suppose. Then I try not to be greedy. The attempt not to be greedy is a movement of time, is becoming.

Bohm: Yes, but even the greed was becoming.

Krishnamurti: Of course. So is it possible, this is the real question, is it possible not to become? Psychologically speaking.

Bohm: Well, it seems that this would require that you should not be anything psychologically. That is, as soon as you define yourself in any way then...

Krishnamurti: No, we will define it in a minute or two.

Bohm: I meant, if I define myself as greedy, or say I am greedy or I am this, or I am that, then either I will want to become something else or to remain what I am - right?

Krishnamurti: Now can I remain what I am? Can I remain not with non-greed but with greed? And greed is not different from me, greed is me.

Bohm: Yes. That will require clarification - the ordinary way of thinking is that I am here and I could either be greedy or not greedy.

Krishnamurti: Of course.

Bohm: As these are attributes which I may or may not have.

Krishnamurti: But the attributes are me.

Bohm: Yes, now again that goes very much against our common language and experience.

Krishnamurti: Of course.

Bohm: But instead we are saying that I am my attributes, which suggests that the thought of attribution creates the ‘me’, right? The sense of ‘me’.

Krishnamurti: All the qualities, the attributes, the virtues, the judgements, the conclusions and opinions are ‘me’.

Bohm: Well, it seems to me that this would have to be perceived immediately as obvious.

Krishnamurti: That is the whole question. To perceive the totality of this movement instantly. Then we come to the point of perception: whether it is possible to perceive - it sounds a little odd, and perhaps a little crazy, but it is not - is it possible to perceive without all the movement of memory? To perceive something directly without the word, without the reaction, without the memories entering into perception?

Bohm: That is a very big question because memory has constantly entered perception. It would raise the question of what is going to stop memory from entering perception?

Krishnamurti: Nothing can stop it. But if we see that the activity of memory is limited, in the very perception of that limitation we have moved out of it into another dimension.

Bohm: Well, it seems to me that you have to perceive the whole of the limitation of memory.

Krishnamurti: Yes, not one part.

Bohm: You can see in general that memory is limited but there are many ways in which this is not obvious. For example, many of our reactions that are not obvious may be memory but we don’t experience them as such. Suppose, say, I experience ‘me’ as being there presently and not as memory. That is the common experience. Suppose I want to become less greedy: I experience greed and I experience the urge to become as an actuality; it may be the result of memory but I say this ‘me’ is the one who remembers, not the other way around, not that memory creates ‘me’.

Krishnamurti: All this really comes down to whether humanity can live without conflict. It basically comes down to that. Can we have peace on this Earth? The activities of thought never bring it about.

Bohm: Yes, it seems clear from what has been said that the activity of thought cannot bring about peace; it inherently, psychologically, brings about conflict.

Krishnamurti: If we once really see that, our whole activity would be totally different.

Bohm: But are you saying then that there is an activity which is not thought, which is beyond thought? And which is not only beyond thought but which does not require the cooperation of thought? That it is possible for this to go on when thought is absent?

Krishnamurti: That is the real point. We have often discussed this, whether there is anything beyond thought. Not something holy, sacred - I am not talking of that. We are asking: is there an activity that is not touched by thought? We are saying there is. And that activity is the highest form of intelligence.

Bohm: Yes, now we have brought in intelligence.

Krishnamurti: I know, I purposely brought it in! So intelligence is not the activity of cunning thought.

Bohm: Well, intelligence can use thought, as you have often said.

Krishnamurti: Intelligence can use thought.

Bohm: Thought can be the action of intelligence - would you put it that way?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Bohm: Or it could be the action of memory?

Krishnamurti: That’s it. Or it is the action born of memory, and memory being limited, thought is therefore limited, and has its own activity which then brings about conflict.

Bohm: I think this would connect up with what people are saying about computers. Every computer must eventually depend on some kind of memory, which is put in, programmed. And that must be limited - right?

Krishnamurti: Of course.

Bohm: Therefore when we operate from memory we are not very different from a computer; the other way around perhaps, the computer is not very different from us!

Krishnamurti: I would say a Hindu has been programmed for the last five thousand years to be a Hindu, or in this country you have been programmed to be British or a Catholic or a Protestant. So we are all programmed to a certain extent.

Bohm: Yes, now you are bringing in the notion of an intelligence that is free of the programme, it is creative perhaps.

Krishnamurti: That’s right. That intelligence has nothing to do with memory and knowledge.

Bohm: It may act in memory and knowledge but it has nothing to do with it.

Krishnamurti: It can act through memory, etc. That’s right. Now, how do you find out whether this has any reality, and is not just imagination and romantic nonsense, how do you find out? To come to that one has to go into the whole question of suffering, whether there is an ending to suffering. As long as suffering and fear and the pursuit of pleasure exist there cannot be love.

Bohm: Yes, well there are many questions there. The first point is that suffering, pleasure, fear, anger, violence and greed are all the response of memory. They are nothing to do with intelligence.

Krishnamurti: Yes, they are all part of thought and memory.

Bohm: As long as they are going on it seems to me that intelligence cannot operate in or through thought.

Krishnamurti: That’s right, so there must be freedom from suffering.

Bohm: Well, that is a very key point.

Krishnamurti: That is really a very serious and deep question: whether it is possible to end suffering, which is the ending of ‘me’.

Bohm: Yes, again, it may seem repetitious but the feeling is that ‘I’ am there and ‘I’ either suffer or don’t, ‘I’ either enjoy things or don’t. Now I think you are saying that suffering arises from thought, it is thought.

Krishnamurti: Through identification, attachment.

Bohm: So what is it that suffers? It seems to me that memory may produce pleasure and then when it doesn’t work it produces pain and suffering.

Krishnamurti: Not only that. Suffering is much more complex, isn’t it? What is suffering? The meaning of the word is to have pain, to have grief, to feel utterly lost, lonely.

Bohm: It seems to me that it is not only pain but a kind of total, very pervasive pain.

Krishnamurti: Suffering is the loss of someone.

Bohm: Or the loss of something very important.

Krishnamurti: Yes, of course. Loss of my wife, of my son, brother, or whatever it is, and the desperate sense of loneliness.

Bohm: Or else just simply the fact that the whole world is going into such a state. It makes everything meaningless, you see.

Krishnamurti: What a lot of suffering all the wars have created! And this has been going on for thousands of years. That is why I am saying we are carrying on with the same pattern of the last five thousand years or more.

Bohm: Yes, and one can easily see that the violence and hatred of war will interfere with intelligence.

Krishnamurti: Obviously.

Bohm: But some people have felt that by going through suffering they become purified, like going through a crucible.

Krishnamurti: I know; that through suffering you learn, you are purified, through suffering your ego vanishes, is dissolved. It isn’t. People have suffered immensely, from so many wars, with so many tears, and from the destructive nature of governments, unemployment, ignorance...

Bohm: ...ignorance of disease, pain, everything. What is suffering really? Why does it destroy or prevent intelligence? What is actually going on?

Krishnamurti: Suffering is a shock - I suffer, I have pain, it is the essence of the ‘me’.

Bohm: Yes, the difficulty with suffering is that it is the ‘me’ that is suffering, and this ‘me’ is really being sorry for itself in some way.

Krishnamurti: My suffering is different from your suffering.

Bohm: It isolates itself, it creates an illusion of some kind.

Krishnamurti: We don’t see suffering is shared by all humanity.

Bohm: Yes, but suppose we do see it is shared by all humanity?

Krishnamurti: Then I begin to question what suffering is. It is not my suffering.

Bohm: Well, that is important. In order to understand the nature of suffering I have to drop this idea that it is my suffering because as long as I believe that I have an illusory notion of the whole thing.

Krishnamurti: And I can never end it.

Bohm: If you are dealing with an illusion you can do nothing with it. You see, we have to come back. Why is suffering the suffering of the many? At first, it seems that I feel toothache, or else I have a loss, or something has happened to me, and the other person seems perfectly happy.

Krishnamurti: Happy, yes. But he is suffering too in his own way.

Bohm: Yes, at the moment he doesn’t see it but he has his problems too.

Krishnamurti: Suffering is common to all humanity.

Bohm: But the fact that it is common is not enough to make it all one.

Krishnamurti: It is actual.

Bohm: Are you saying that the suffering of mankind is all one, inseparable?

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is what I have been saying.

Bohm: As is the consciousness of man?

Krishnamurti: Yes, that’s right.

Bohm: That when anybody suffers the whole of mankind is suffering.

Krishnamurti: The whole point is we have suffered from the beginning of time and we haven’t solved it.

Bohm: But what you have said is that the reason we haven’t solved it is because we are treating it as personal or as in a small group, and that is an illusion. And any attempt to deal with an illusion cannot solve anything.

Krishnamurti: Thought cannot solve anything psychologically.

Bohm: Because you can say that thought itself divides. Thought is limited and is not able to see that this suffering is all one. And in that way divides it up as mine and yours.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Bohm: And that creates illusion, which can only multiply suffering. Now, it seems to me that the statement that the suffering of mankind is one is inseparable from the statement that the consciousness of mankind is one.

Krishnamurti: Sir, the world is me, I am the world.

Bohm: You have often said that.

Krishnamurti: Yes, but we have divided it up into the British Earth and the French Earth and all the rest of it!

Bohm: Do you mean by the world, the physical world, or the world of society?

Krishnamurti: The world of society, the psychological world, primarily.

Bohm: So we say the world of society, of human beings, is one, and when I say I am that world, what does it mean?

Krishnamurti: The world is not different from me.

Bohm: The world and I are one, we are inseparable.

Krishnamurti: Yes. And what that requires, is real meditation, you must feel this, it is not just a verbal statement, it is an actuality. I am my brother’s keeper.

Bohm: Many religions have said that.

Krishnamurti: That is just a verbal statement; they don’t keep it, they don’t do it in their hearts.

Bohm: Perhaps a few have done it but in general it is not being done.

Krishnamurti: I don’t know if anybody has done it, we human beings haven’t done it. Our religions have actually prevented it!

Bohm: Because of division, every religion having its own beliefs and its own organization?

Krishnamurti: Of course, its own gods and its own saviours. So after all this, is that intelligence actual? You understand my question? Or is it some kind of fanciful projection, hoping that it will solve our problems? It is not to me, it is an actuality. Because the ending of suffering means love.

Bohm: Now before we go on, let’s clear up a point about ‘me’. You have just said ‘it is not to me’. Now in some sense it seems that you are still defining an individual. Is that right?

Krishnamurti: Yes, I am using the word ‘I’ as a means of communication.

Bohm: But what does it mean? Let’s say there are two people, ‘A’ who sees the way you do and ‘B’ who does not. That seems to create a division between ‘A’ and ‘B’.

Krishnamurti: That’s right, but ‘B’ creates the division.

Bohm: Why?

Krishnamurti: What is the relationship between the two?

Bohm: Well, ‘B’ is creating the division by saying, ‘I am a separate person’, but it may confuse ‘B’ further when ‘A’ says, ‘it’s not that way to me’ - right?

Krishnamurti: That is the whole point, isn’t it, in relationship? You feel that you are not separate, and that you really have this sense of love and compassion, and I haven’t got it. I haven’t even perceived or gone into this question. What is your relationship to me? You have a relationship with me but I haven’t any relationship with you.

Bohm: Yes, I think one could say that the person who hasn’t seen is, psychologically, almost living in a dream world, and therefore the dream world is not related to the world of being awake.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Bohm: But the fellow who is awake can at least perhaps awaken the other fellow.

Krishnamurti: You are awake, I am not. Then your relationship with me is very clear. But I have no relationship with you, I cannot. I insist on division and you don’t.

Bohm: Yes, in some way we have to say that the consciousness of mankind has divided itself, it is all one, but it has divided itself by thought. That is why we are in this situation.

Krishnamurti: That is why - all the problems that humanity has now, psychologically as well as in other ways, are the result of thought. And we are pursuing the same pattern of thought, and thought will never solve any of these problems. So there is another kind of instrument, which is intelligence.

Bohm: That opens up an entirely different subject, and you also mentioned love and compassion as well.

Krishnamurti: Without love and compassion there is no intelligence. And you cannot be compassionate if you are attached to some religion, like an animal tied to a post.

Bohm: Well, as soon as your self is threatened then it cannot...

Krishnamurti: Of course. But you see, the self hides behind...

Bohm: ...other things, such as noble ideals.

Krishnamurti: Yes, it has immense capacity to hide itself. So what is the future of mankind? From what one observes it is leading to destruction.

Bohm: That is the way it seems to be going.

Krishnamurti: Very gloomy, grim, dangerous and if one has children what is their future? To enter into all this and go through all the misery of it all? So education becomes extraordinarily important. But now education is merely the accumulation of knowledge.

Bohm: Every instrument that man has invented, discovered or developed has been turned toward destruction.

Krishnamurti: Absolutely. They are destroying nature, there are very few tigers now.

Bohm: Forests and agricultural land are being destroyed too.

Krishnamurti: Nobody seems to care.

Bohm: Well, most people are just immersed in their plans to save themselves but others have plans to save humanity. I think also there is a tendency toward despair implicit in what is happening now in that people don’t think anything can be done.

Krishnamurti: Yes, and if they think something can be done they form little groups and little theories.

Bohm: Well, there are those who are very confident in what they are doing and those who...

Krishnamurti: Most Prime Ministers are very confident. But they don’t really know what they are doing.

Bohm: But most people don’t have much confidence in what they are doing.

Krishnamurti: I know. But if you have tremendous confidence, I may accept your confidence and go with you. So what then is the future of mankind? I wonder if anybody is concerned about it? Or is each person, or each group, concerned only with its own survival?

Bohm: Well, I think the first concern has almost always been with the survival of the individual or the group. That has been the history of mankind.

Krishnamurti: And therefore perpetual wars, perpetual insecurity.

Bohm: But this, as you said, is the result of thought making the mistake, on an incomplete basis, of identifying the self with the group, and so on.

Krishnamurti: Sir, you happen to listen to all this, you agree to all this, you see the truth of all this. Those in power will not even listen to you, they are creating more and more misery, the world is becoming more and more dangerous. What is the point of you and I agreeing and seeing something true? This is what people are asking: what is the point of you and I seeing something to be true and what effect has it?

Bohm: Well it seems to me if we think in terms of the effects, we are bringing in the very thing that is behind the trouble - time. That is, the first response would be we must quickly get in and do something to change the course of events.

Krishnamurti: Therefore form a society, foundation, organization and all the rest of it.

Bohm: You see, our mistake is to feel that we must think of something, and that thought is incomplete. We don’t really know what is going on, and people have made theories about it, but they don’t know.

Krishnamurti: No, but if that is the wrong question, then as a human being, who is mankind, what is my responsibility, apart from effect and all the rest of it?

Bohm: Yes, we can’t look toward effects. But it is the same as with ‘A’ and ‘B’, that ‘A’ sees and ‘B’ does not. Now suppose ‘A’ sees something and most of the rest of mankind does not. Then it seems, one could say mankind is in some way dreaming, it is asleep.

Krishnamurti: It is caught in illusion.

Bohm: And the point is that, if somebody sees something, his responsibility is to help awaken the others out of the illusion.

Krishnamurti: That is just it. I mean this has been the problem. That is why the Buddhists have projected the idea of the Bodhisattva, who is compassionate, the essence of all compassion, and who is waiting to save humanity. It sounds nice, it is a happy feeling that there is somebody doing this. But in actuality we won’t do anything that is not, both psychologically and physically, comfortable, satisfying, secure.

Bohm: Well, that is basically the source of the illusion.

Krishnamurti: How does one make others see all this? They haven’t time, the energy, even the inclination, they want to be amused. How does one make ‘X’ see this whole thing so clearly that he says, ‘all right, I have got it, I will work, and I see I am responsible’, and all the rest of it? I think there is the tragedy of those who see and those who do not.

Brockwood Park, 18 October 1984

Who Is the Experiencer?

Iris Murdoch, philosopher and novelist.

Iris Murdoch: I have a lot of questions so I will just start with one of particular interest and see where we go.

It is about the word ‘experience’ which you sometimes use in your writings as representing something that you think we should in some sense overcome. You seem to connect the idea of experience with the notion of preconceived attitudes or dogmas or beliefs which impede a kind of being that you would connect with a creative present existence. I don’t entirely understand this. It seems to me impossible to entirely...

Krishnamurti: ...wipe out experience.

Murdoch: Yes, to discount or escape from experience. I would like just to stick to the term ‘experience’ because perhaps there is a particular sense you want to attach to it. It is such a very general word, it seems to describe the continuity of consciousness that is simply characteristic of being human. Perhaps you could say something about that.

Krishnamurti: I don’t quite know what you mean by experience. One can experience what one desires.

Murdoch: You mean imagining it?

Krishnamurti: Yes. Also, one can experience according to your conditioning. If I am a devout Buddhist, I can experience the state of that consciousness which was supposed to have been Buddha’s.

Murdoch: Well, that is a rather special sort of experience, isn’t it?

Krishnamurti: Yes, but I am just questioning what we mean by experience. Say I experience anger. Is there a difference between the experience and the experiencer?

Murdoch: Well, this is a difficult question of usage because the word ‘experience’ in English describes something fairly vague. It can mean something momentary, either, ‘I had a strange experience yesterday’, or it can mean the continuity of your conscious life and your relationship to your past. But I think what you mean by it is something that, as it were, collects your past. At one point I think you have described desire as experience, whereas you say that love is not experience.

Krishnamurti: Love cannot be experience.

Murdoch: Could you explain what the distinction is?

Krishnamurti: Could we go into the question of who experiences the whole thing, anything, whether it is the experience of something imagined, or the experience of one’s past tradition and images, and so on?

Murdoch: You are asking, who is the experiencer?

Krishnamurti: Yes, who is experiencing?

Murdoch: This is also a difficult question, isn’t it? If one were to ask a passer-by in the street he would say the ‘individual’.

Krishnamurti: Yes, ‘I’ am experiencing.

Murdoch: These experiences belong to ‘me’.

Krishnamurti: I had an experience of an accident this morning in a car. I experience so many things.

Murdoch: But then, if one were to pursue the matter beyond that kind of answer, one might say well of course one must distinguish between different kinds of experience. I can think of, say, three kinds immediately: there is the experience of my past life, we say of somebody, ‘he is an experienced man’, meaning he has a lot of experiences of a particular kind, and then we would also say that experience is just the continuity of my consciousness, going away into the past.

Krishnamurti: Or the continuity of one’s consciousness. What do you mean by the word ‘consciousness’?

Murdoch: Well, let’s pursue the matter in this way; one would say consciousness differs at different times. And the meaning of the word ‘experience’ I would think would differ, depending on whether you were talking about just ordinary life. Let’s put it this way: partly you were sort of imposing yourself on the world and saying ‘I am doing this, I am doing that’, and this would be perhaps experience. But also there might be an experience where you aren’t really present.

Krishnamurti: That’s it, that’s just it. Where the experiencer is not, is there an experience that you can then remember and say, ‘this is it’?

Murdoch: Well, I would think that people have what I would call selfless experience when, for instance, they are looking at a great work of art.

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Murdoch: I am not sure whether one could say this if they are with somebody they love very much. I think these two cases are very different. What do you think?

Krishnamurti: I would like first to go into the question, if I may, of who is experiencing all this? Whether it is ordinary things, or the most complicated forms of experiences, or so-called spiritual experiences. Who is it that is always experiencing? Is the experiencer different from the experience?

Murdoch: Well one would normally say so, wouldn’t one, because one may believe in the continuity of an individual person.

Krishnamurti: Yes, that is what is commonly held. Now we are going to question that: is the thinker different from his thoughts?

Murdoch: Again, we would usually say so because one could say, ‘I order my thoughts’. This assumes that I am deciding, that I collect my thoughts.

Krishnamurti: Yes, but is that I, who orders his thoughts, different from his thoughts? He may order them, he may discipline them, he may control them, he might say, ‘this is right, this is wrong, this must be done, that must not be done’, but is the controller, the person who disciplines, brings order, different from the things that he is ordering?

Murdoch: Well, let’s make a distinction here between ordinary language in a law court where somebody is held responsible for something they have done. They can’t say, ‘well I am a different person now’, or something like that. This is the ordinary sense of the continuity of the individual and somebody being the subject. But leaving that aside, one doesn’t have to be a philosopher or hold a religious view to think that one is divided, a divided person.

Krishnamurti: That’s it.

Murdoch: And there are times when one part of you disapproves of another part.

Krishnamurti: The dualistic process... is there a difference between - we come back to the old question - the good and the bad?

Murdoch: Well, nothing could be more fundamental, yes, I mean this seems to me the nature of the real world.

Krishnamurti: I know. The real world is division: we have divided the good and the bad, and the thinker from the thought, the experiencer from the experience.

Murdoch: Yes, this would follow in that if you condemn yourself for doing something then you are divided.

Krishnamurti: ‘I should not’, ‘I must’, ‘I will become’, and all the rest of it, this breeds division in oneself. I would like to ask again, if I may, is that experiencer different from the thing he is experiencing or the thinker different from his thoughts?

Murdoch: Well, if this is an appeal to how I think about myself, I would say - leaving aside the common-sense, the ordinary-language view - sometimes yes and sometimes no. I mean sometimes one is consciously judging oneself, dividing oneself, sometimes there is nothing except a single being or something.

Krishnamurti: A single movement. So is not the experiencer the same as the experience?

Murdoch: Well, it sometimes seems so.

Krishnamurti: So when we say, ‘I am envious’, there is a division, then ‘I’ try to control or rationalize my envy, to justify or suppress it and so on, but the ‘I’ is envy, not separate from it.

Murdoch: Well, I would have thought that it is and it isn’t. From what I have read and understood there are two things that you say; and I don’t understand how they connect or harmonize. One of the things that I liked very much was this: if I think that I am envious, say, the word ‘envy’ suggests something bad so I may want not to be envious. If I see this, I must start, not with a kind of ideal self that doesn’t exist but in my real being, which is the envious person. I feel great sympathy with this. But then you also say that there is no process involved here, I must be good, not become good, and that the idea of becoming good is in some way an illusion.

Krishnamurti: That’s right.

Murdoch: Perhaps you could explain this, I mean it seems to me that in the first case you are suggesting that I must start from a point that is a long way from my conclusion, my conclusion being to become unenvious. In the second case you are saying that there is no process of becoming.

Krishnamurti: For me there is no psychological becoming at all.

Murdoch: Yes, this is what I don’t understand.

Krishnamurti: Let us go into it. First of all let’s come to this point: we have divided the good from the bad both in the world and in ourselves. Right?

Murdoch: But you don’t dispute, you don’t object to this?

Krishnamurti: I don’t refute it, I am just looking at it. Is the bad related to the good or is the good totally divorced from the bad, so that they are not related at all? If they are related the good will still be part of the bad.

Murdoch: Well, if you are asking me if I agree with that, I am not sure. It seems to me we think about good and bad in several different ways, don’t we? We think of bad grading into good as if it were a spectrum, with goodness here and badness there.

Krishnamurti: Yes, continuing into the bad.

Murdoch: A continuing. We also think of good - if we think of it as perfection - as being really outside the world altogether.

Krishnamurti: Not as perfection - I mean being good, whole, good health, a good man, the word ‘good’ in that sense.

Murdoch: Let’s say ‘a good man’ then.

Krishnamurti: Is that good part of the bad? Does the good know the bad? Is the good the outcome of the bad? If it is the outcome then it is still part of the bad. It’s like a child being born; it’s still part of the mother.

Murdoch: Yes, some people would say that they are opposites that exist in relation to each other.

Krishnamurti: Now, I ask: are they opposite? Or are they totally unrelated?

Murdoch: Well, there is a very clear difference between a bad man and a good man. So in that sense they are very different. On the other hand in a human being good and bad grade into each other, and sometimes you don’t know which is which.

Krishnamurti: No, that is what I am questioning, that is what I would like to discuss with you. I mean to me the good is totally divorced from the bad, just as love is not related to hate.

Murdoch: Yes. I mean in the ordinary fallen human condition of course love often occasions hate.

Krishnamurti: Of course.

Murdoch: Whereas you say that love is not related to hate, that it is an entirely different kind of concept.

Krishnamurti: Totally. Love has no feeling about hate, it has no relation to hate, it does not encompass or embrace hate.

Murdoch: Wait a minute. Let me ask a supplementary question. Would you say the same about love and desire?

Krishnamurti: Yes, I would.

Murdoch: You regard desire as something connected with psychological becoming?

Krishnamurti: Yes.

Murdoch: Whereas love is...

Krishnamurti: ...something entirely different.

Murdoch: Now, how does this different thing come to one? I might say now, ‘why should it concern me?’ What am I to do about it?

Krishnamurti: It is simple enough. There is conflict. Desire always brings conflict, but love can never bring conflict. Love has no conflict, it has no sense of conflict.

Murdoch: You are using the word ‘love’ in an ideal sense, which is unusual.

Krishnamurti: No, the brain is the entire centre of desire, feeling, anxiety, pain, loneliness. The consciousness is all that, the beliefs, fears, sorrow, loneliness, anxiety, the whole...

Murdoch: ...the psychological being.

Krishnamurti: Yes, the psychological structure, confusion. That is the brain. And love is not part of the brain because it is something outside that.

Murdoch: Yes, this comes back to your saying you don’t experience love in the way in which you experience desire.

Krishnamurti: I can’t experience something that is so.

Murdoch: In ordinary parlance we speak of a ‘jealous’ love or something like that. But that is not what we are talking about. We are talking about some sort of absolute - I can’t think of the right word here - love. But suppose I dearly love somebody in one might say not a bad way but in a good way, as it were, would you want to say that this is not part of any psychological process in my mind?

Krishnamurti: No, I would say: if when I say I love you there is any tinge of attachment, jealousy, any shadow of conflict, then it is not the real thing.

Murdoch: Yes, all right. I was brought up as a Christian, so there is a lot of the Christian way of looking in me, although I don’t believe in God or the divinity of

Christ. But in Christianity there would be an idea of divine or perfect love, which is something that we don’t normally achieve perhaps at all.

Krishnamurti: I don’t see why not. Because if I am not jealous, I won’t be jealous. When there is no sense of attachment to another person, that doesn’t mean lack of love.

Murdoch: Well, in what we would call in ordinary parlance a virtuous love, where you are not hurting anybody else by loving this person, and you are not possessive, unreasonable and so on, there is attachment. I mean inevitably if the person dies...

Krishnamurti: Wait a minute, that is a different question. Why are we attached to anything? If I am attached to this house...

Murdoch: I would, I think, take a different view of the notion of desire. I would think that becoming good - to use this phrase that perhaps you would want to exclude - is a matter of purifying one’s desires, of having ‘good’ desires, desiring something that is good. Now in loving somebody I would have felt that the element of desire was present.

Krishnamurti: Let’s look at desire. What is desire?

Murdoch: Well, there again one would say there are low desires and there are high desires.

Krishnamurti: Yes, but what is the origin, the beginning of desire? Why has desire become such an extraordinarily important part of our life?

Murdoch: Desire is certainly connected with the future.

Krishnamurti: With the future.

Murdoch: It is connected with time.

Krishnamurti: Of course, with time.

Murdoch: Because I desire something that is absent. Let’s take examples. I might desire to be frightfully rich, or I might desire to study a subject and become good at it.

Krishnamurti: Good at the piano.

Murdoch: Well, let’s say good at mathematics, to acquire knowledge.

Krishnamurti: Yes, of course.

Murdoch: And I might say I love my subject, I love what I am studying.

Krishnamurti: No, what I am asking is: what is desire? How does it come about? Why does it control us so strongly? After all a monk, or one of the Indian sannyasis, their whole idea is to suppress or to transmute desire.

Murdoch: Well, transmute, yes. I would rather use the word ‘transmute’.

Krishnamurti: To transmute means that there is an entity who transmutes it.

Murdoch: Yes, and there is a process of transmuting, and a discipline or a training, or something like that.

Krishnamurti: Which is only a subtle form of suppression, a subtle form of organizing desire, or saying desire for God is good.

Murdoch: Or desire for riches is bad.

Krishnamurti: And desire for possessions is bad. So we are not discussing the objects of desire, whether it is God, or power, whether to become a rich man or a prime minister, but what is desire? How does it take shape in us?

Murdoch: Well, whether there can be love without desire I am not sure. If one thinks perhaps of some kind of perfect love the notion of desire would have changed so much that perhaps you would have to exclude it. At a more ordinary but good level, if I desire to become well educated or something...
